et al., 2008), or suppression of NK cell function (Udoji et al., 2010). 
In another study in which occupationally exposed individuals were 
evaluated for cytokine levels, DDE was associated with suppression 
of IL-2 and IFN-γ, and enhancement of IL-4, suggesting an imbal-
ance of Th1/Th2 populations (Daniel et al., 2002). A study in mice 
demonstrates macrophage polarization imbalance (Mangum et al., 
2016), suggesting that alteration of immune cell populations might 
account for the immune suppression by DDE.
Dieldrin was used from the 1950s until 1987 when its use 
was banned due to environmental and human health concerns. The 
humoral immune response to both T-cell-dependent and T-cell-
independent antigens is suppressed following exposure to dieldrin 
(Bernier et al., 1987). Macrophage functions are also depressed in 
dieldrin-exposed animals, which correlates with the increased sus-
ceptibility of dieldrin-exposed animals to murine hepatitis virus (a 
virus that targets macrophages) (Krzystyniak et al., 1985). CMI is 
also suppressed in mice following dieldrin exposure as measured 
by a suppression of the MLR and graft-versus-host disease (Hugo 
et al., 1988a, 1988b). Dieldrin also induces inflammation under cer-
tain conditions, as dieldrin enhances the pro-inflammatory cytokine 
IL-8 in vitro, increases superoxide production in vitro (Mangum et 
al., 2015), and induces neutrophilic inflammation in vivo (Pelletier 
et al., 2001).
Chlordane refers to a group of structurally related chemicals 
used to control termites. The principal constituents of chlordane are 
heptachlor, α-chlordane, γ-chlordane, α-nonachlor, β-nonachlor, 
α-chlordene, β-chlordene, and γ-chlordene. Definitive immune 
suppression produced by chlordane was first reported by Spyker-
Cranmer et al. (1982). In utero exposure results in decreased DTH 
responses in mice with no deficit in antibody production to sRBC. 
This effect is correlated with an increase in resistance to influenza 
infection because DTH contributes to the pathology of the infection 
(Menna et al., 1985). In contrast to observations from mice exposed 
in utero, exposure of adult mice to chlordane does not result in any 
changes to several immune parameters, including AFC response 
to sRBC, MLR, DTH, or mitogenic lymphoproliferation. In rats, 
immunomodulatory effects of three constituents of the chlordane 
mixture (i.e. technical chlordane, cis-nonachlor, or trans-nonachlor) 
were analyzed following 28-day oral dosing. The most profound 
effect was increased lymphocyte numbers with decreased NK cell 
activity and proliferation in response to Salmonella typhimurium 
(Tryphonas et al., 2003). The increase in cell numbers is consis-
tent with a study conducted in Japan in which high concentrations 
of chlordane in breast milk increased CD3+ and CD8+ T-cell per-
centages in infants at approximately 10 months of age (Nagayama 
et al., 2007).
Organotins  Trisubstituted organotins such as tributyltin oxide 
are widely used as biocides and have recently been recognized as 
producing some immunotoxic effects. The most prominent action 
of tributyltin oxide is the induction of profound but reversible thy-
mic atrophy. Studies by Vos et al. (1984) demonstrated a decrease 
in cellularity in the spleen, bone marrow, and thymus. The decrease 
in splenic cellularity was associated with a concomitant loss of T 
lymphocytes (Vos et al., 1984). In Jurkat T cells, tributyltin oxide 
increases intracellular calcium and NFAT translocation to the 
nucleus, but the increase in proliferation is not sustained, and at 
later times, there is decreased proliferation and increased expres-
sion of several apoptotic genes (Katika et al., 2011). In mouse thy-
mocytes, there are increased annexin-V+ and Fas ligand–expressing 
cells following oral administration of tributyltin chloride (Chen 
et al., 2011), providing additional evidence that thymic atrophy is 
due to apoptosis.
Another sensitive target of organotin compounds is the NK 
cell. Van Loveren et al. (1990) observed suppressed lung NK cyto-
toxicity in rats exposed orally to tributyltin oxide. The mechanism 
for suppressed NK cell activity was investigated using human NK 
cells, which demonstrates an increase in intracellular calcium and 
increased expression of various AP-1 transcription factors (i.e., jun 
and fos) (Lane et al., 2009; Person and Whalen, 2010). However, 
overall, there is decreased AP-1 binding activity, which the authors 
suggest is due to increased prevalence of jun–jun homodimers, 
which exhibit lower affinity for the AP-1 site than other com-
plexes, such as jun–fos heterodimers (Person and Whalen, 2010). 
Moreover, the mechanism by which dibutyltin and tributyltin sup-
pressed NK cell activity might be due to its ability to interact with 
sulfhydryl groups, because glutathione treatment reverses the sup-
pression (Powell et al., 2009). Additional papers investigating direct 
effects of dibutyltin and tributyltin in different fractions of human 
PBMCs demonstrated differential effects of the toxicants depend-
ing on the cell composition (Hurt et al., 2013; Brown and Whalen, 
2015; Lawrence et al., 2015). For instance, tributyltin robustly 
suppresses TNF-α production from highly purified NK cells, 
but this effect is lost in a T cell/NK cell mixed population (Hurt 
et al., 2013). These results demonstrate the importance of consid-
ering immune cell–cell interactions in understanding mechanisms 
of immunotoxicity. Recently, tributyltin was also demonstrated to 
impair stem cell to B-cell development in vitro through activation 
of apoptosis (Baker et al., 2017).
Carbamates  Carbamates, which include carbaryl (Sevin), aldi-
carb, mancozeb, and sodium methyldithiocarbamate, are used 
primarily as insecticides. Similar to the organophosphates, the 
mechanism of action for the neurotoxic effects involves inhibi-
tion of acetylcholinesterase. In an evaluation of humoral immunity 
following a 2-week exposure to carbaryl in rats, suppression of

Toxic Responses of the Immune System
Chapter 12
679
the IgM AFC response to sRBC is observed following inhalation 
exposure, but not oral or dermal exposure (Ladics et al., 1994). 
Following oral exposure in rats, carbaryl suppresses splenic prolif-
erative responses, but enhances pulmonary inflammation and IgE, 
in response to house dust mite, suggesting that carbaryl exhibits tis-
sue-specific effects (Dong et al., 1998). The mechanism by which 
carbaryl is suppressive involves inhibition of LPS-induced signal-
ing in macrophages (Ohnishi et al., 2008).
Pruett et al. (1992a) evaluated the immunotoxicity of sodium 
methyldithiocarbamate, and observed decreased thymus weight, 
depletion of the CD4+/CD8+ population of thymocytes, and pro-
found suppression of NK cell activity following both oral and der-
mal exposure. They also determined that the mechanism by which 
sodium methyldithiocarbamate alters cytokine production from 
peritoneal macrophages involves inhibition of MAP kinase and 
NF-κB activity via TLR4 (Pruett et al., 2005; Deng et al., 2013). 
The mechanism of cytokine alteration involves depletion of gluta-
thione, alteration of copper-dependent proteins, induction of stress 
(Pruett et al., 2006), and suppression of interferon-related genes 
(Deng et al., 2013).
Atrazine  Atrazine is a herbicide applied to various agricultural 
crops to control broad leaf weeds. It is widely used in the United 
States and it has been detected in soils and groundwater because of 
its resistance to degradation. Similar to other chemicals discussed, 
atrazine exhibits immunomodulatory effects. Using offspring of 
dams treated with atrazine and challenged with antigen, atrazine 
induces elevations in T-cell proliferation, cytolytic activity, and 
antigen-specific B cells in male offspring (Rowe et al., 2006). In 
contrast, young mice directly administered atrazine orally for 14 
days exhibit suppressed thymic weight, spleen and thymic cel-
lularity, and B-cell populations, although CD4+ T-cell numbers 
increased (Filipov et al., 2005). Similarly, in adult mice, it was 
confirmed that atrazine suppresses thymic weight, and also sup-
presses splenic weight and decreases the host resistance of the mice 
to B16F10 melanoma tumors (Karrow et al., 2005). In vitro, atra-
zine suppresses cell surface expression of MHCII, CD86, CD11b, 
and CD11c using a mouse DC line (Pinchuk et al., 2007). Although 
the exact mechanism by which atrazine-induced immune suppres-
sion occurred is unclear; atrazine treatment of mice does induce 
corticosterone levels, indicating that activation of the hypotha-
lamic-pituitary-adrenal axis might be involved (Pruett et al., 2003). 
Atrazine has also been shown to induce lymphocyte apoptosis in 
vivo (Zhang et al., 2011) and in vitro (Lee et al., 2016).
Metals
Generally speaking, metals target multiple organ systems and exert 
their toxic effects via an interaction of the free metal with targets, such 
as enzyme systems, membranes, or cellular organelles. In consider-
ing the immunotoxicity of most metals, early studies as reviewed 
by Koller (1980) established metals as immunomodulators, but not 
consistently immunosuppressive as will be explored below for spe-
cific metals. However, the most consistent finding in these studies 
is increased susceptibility to pathogens. Additionally, a consistent 
finding in human and rodent studies from exposure to the various 
metals found in tobacco smoke is the induction of pro-inflammatory 
cytokines (reviewed by Milnerowicz et al. [2015]). Furthermore, as 
with most immunotoxic agents, it is important to note that exposures 
to metals are likely not single exposures, although one metal might 
dominate depending on the exposure conditions (e.g., high levels of 
mercury in fish or high levels of lead from paint).
Similar to pesticides, there is certainly a large body of literature 
describing the metal-induced immune alterations in mammalian, 
amphibian, and fish animal models, but again, the studies described 
here will focus on those that provide mechanistic information 
related to epidemiological effects in humans and will focus on the 
four best studied immunotoxic metals: lead, arsenic, mercury, and 
cadmium. Moreover, there has been a substantial increase in efforts 
to understand immune alterations following exposure to metal-con-
taining nanomaterials. For this, the reader is referred to Chap. 29, 
“Nanoparticle Toxicology” and several recent reviews (Zolnik et al., 
2010; Kononenko et al., 2015; Luo et al., 2015).
Lead  Studies support various immunotoxic effects of lead, rang-
ing from decreased bacterial and viral resistance to increased hyper-
sensitivity and autoimmunity (reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink 
[2006a], Mishra [2009], and Kasten-Jolly and Lawrence [2014]). 
Perhaps mediating the decreased resistance to pathogens, lead has 
been shown to decrease macrophage production of nitric oxide 
(Farrer et al., 2008) and to impair immune recognition by decreasing 
the ability of macrophages to process and present antigens to anti-
gen-primed T cells (reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink [2006a] 
and Kasten-Jolly and Lawrence [2014]). Additionally, lead shifts 
the T-cell balance from Th1 to Th2 leading to increased Th2 and 
decreased Th1 cytokine expression profiles (i.e., increased IL-4 and 
decreased IFN-γ), which could promote M2 vs. M1 macrophages 
and contribute to a decreased ability to fight intracellular patho-
gens (reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink [2006a] and Kasten-
Jolly and Lawrence [2014]). The mechanism by which lead induces 
a Th2 switch has been suggested to involve lead-induced altera-
tions in bone marrow–derived DC populations that preferentially 
stimulate Th2 differentiation (Gao et al., 2007). A lead-induced 
Th2 shift may contribute to the consistent finding of increased IgE, 
whereas effects on the AFC response and other Ig isotypes are less 
consistent, perhaps due to differences in experimental conditions 
(reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink [2006a]). Correspondingly, 
it has been hypothesized that lead exposure might contribute to the 
development of asthma (Dietert and Piepenbrink, 2006a), a pre-
dominantly Th2-mediated disease. This theory is consistent with the 
observed increase in IgE production and asthma incidence in lead-
exposed children (Lutz et al., 1999; Karmaus et al., 2005; Hsiao et 
al., 2011). Interestingly, Lutz et al. (2012) suggest that the increase 
in IgE in lead-exposed children is more related to a co-exposure 
with cigarette smoke exposure rather than due to the lead-exposure 
alone. Furthermore, children living near electronic waste sites in 
China exhibited decreased NK cell numbers correlated to blood 
lead levels (Zhang et al., 2016c). Corresponding to the decreases 
in Th1 responses and antigen recognition and processing, several 
studies have identified lead-induced inhibition of DTH responses 
(reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink [2006a]). Lead exposure 
is also associated with inflammation and increased expression of 
inflammatory cytokines, the generation of ROS, and tissue damage 
(reviewed by Dietert and Piepenbrink [2006a]). Specific to hema-
topoietic stem cells, lead-induced mitochondrial dysfunction and 
ROS generation resulted in apoptosis and loss of stem cells (Liu et 
al., 2015b). Additionally, in vitro and in vivo acute and subchronic 
(8 weeks) studies support an increase in neuroinflammation and 
neuronal death mediated by lead-induced activation of microglia 
and secretion of inflammatory cytokines (Liu et al., 2012; Kumawat 
et al., 2014). In contrast, subchronic (6 weeks) exposure to lead (or 
cadmium) in an animal model of colitis decreased inflammatory 
cytokines and signs of colitis (Breton et al., 2016). Furthermore, 
a cross-sectional analysis of data from the National Health and 
Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES) found an association 
between blood lead levels and an increased risk of chronic infec-
tions (i.e., Helicobacter pylori, Toxoplasma gondii, and hepatitis B 
virus) (Krueger and Wade, 2016). In summary, lead clearly alters

680
Target Organ Toxicity
Unit IV
immune function resulting mostly in increased pathogen suscep-
tibility, tissue inflammation, and the potential for hypersensitivity 
and autoimmune reactions.
Arsenic  A challenge in understanding the mechanisms by which 
arsenic is immunotoxic is that in addition to inconsistencies that 
arise from route of administration, concentrations/doses, and spe-
cies/strain differences, the speciation of arsenic plays a signifi-
cant role in arsenic toxicity (reviewed by Abdul et al. [2015] and 
Sattar et al. [2016]). Arsenic compounds affect human immune 
function. For instance, in environmentally exposed children, there 
was a correlation between total arsenic in urine (inorganic and 
arsenic metabolites) and superoxide anion production from stim-
ulated monocytes (Luna et al., 2010) and in adult smelter work-
ers, higher levels of urinary arsenic correlated with increased lipid 
peroxidation and lower vitamin E levels in blood (Escobar et al., 
2010). These results suggest that arsenic exposure causes oxi-
dative stress and in fact, using the THP-1 monocytic cell line, it 
was shown that arsenic trioxide decreased glutathione levels and 
induced heme oxygenase, which is often induced to combat oxi-
dative stress (Wang et al., 2011). There are also several studies in 
which human PBMC from unexposed individuals were treated in 
vitro with arsenic compounds. The percentage of IL-2-producing 
T cells in human PBMC was suppressed following treatment with 
sodium arsenite (Galicia et al., 2003). An additional study showed 
that sodium arsenite suppressed IL-2 production while also stim-
ulating heme oxygenase in purified T cells, indicating that the 
direct T-cell impairment by arsenic occurs in parallel with oxida-
tive stress (Martin-Chouly et al., 2011). Additionally, arsenic tri-
oxide decreased the proliferation of alloreactive CD8+ memory 
T cells and prevented allograft rejection in an animal model (Li 
et al., 2015a). Humoral immune function is also compromised by 
arsenic compounds, as suggested by anti-sRBC AFC responses in 
mice (Sikorski et al., 1991; Burchiel et al., 2009). Interestingly, 
in spite of a fairly extensive database showing suppression of 
antibody responses in murine models, in purified human B cells 
stimulated with CD40L, sodium arsenite did not suppress IgM pro-
duction (Lu et al., 2009). However, treatment of primary human 
macrophages with arsenic trioxide resulted in decreased expression 
of CD14 (a PRR and co-receptor of TLRs), altered morphology, 
and suppressed adhesion (Lemarie et al., 2006). The alterations in 
macrophage function were dependent on arsenic trioxide–induced 
activation of RhoA (small GTPase and Ras homolog) (Lemarie 
et al., 2006). Similar results (increased RhoA activation, altered mor-
phology, and cell adhesion) were observed in macrophages isolated 
from humans chronically exposed to arsenic (Banerjee et al., 2009). 
At higher concentrations, arsenic trioxide also induced apoptosis 
of macrophages through inhibition of NF-κB signaling (Lemarie 
et al., 2006). Induction of macrophage apoptosis is the basis for 
the therapeutic use of arsenic trioxide to treat acute promyelocytic 
leukemia. Additionally, studies with both rodent and human mac-
rophages treated with arsenic support decreased phagocytosis, NO 
and superoxide production, chemotaxis, and antigen presentation, 
which may account for the observed increases in susceptibility to 
infections and tumors (Bishayi and Sengupta, 2003; Arkusz et al., 
2005; Banerjee et al., 2009). Correspondingly, an increased risk of 
fungal infections was associated with arsenic exposure in drink-
ing water in two Taiwanese study cohorts (Hsu et al., 2016). In 
addition to altered macrophage function, a recent study evaluated 
the effects of sodium arsenite on porcine macrophage–derived DC 
and identified decreased phagocytosis and decreased ability to acti-
vate and induce T-cell proliferation; however, the pro-inflammatory 
cytokines IL-6 and IL-1β were increased (Mehrzad et al., 2017).
Mice exposed to arsenite in drinking water exhibited differ-
ences in speciation in the bone marrow, spleen, thymus, and plasma, 
whereas the bone marrow and thymus demonstrated increased 
and dose-dependent sensitivity to DNA damage as compared to 
the spleen (Xu et al., 2016a). Correspondingly, thymic evaluation 
following in vivo exposure to sodium arsenite in drinking water 
demonstrated increased oxidative stress and thymocyte apoptosis, 
which was associated with increased NF-κB nuclear localization 
and Cox-2 levels. Additionally, there appeared to be a shift toward 
the development of nTregs within the thymus (Choudhury et al., 
2016). Interestingly, serum cytokines showed an increase in IL-2 
(suggested to promote nTreg development), IL-17A, and IL-10, 
and a decrease in TNF-α and IL-6 with increased concentration 
of sodium arsenite (Choudhury et al., 2016). A second study dem-
onstrated similar results in that sodium arsenite decreased CD4+ 
T cells and increased oxidative stress as well as factors involved 
in the development of nTregs; but in contrast, this study also iden-
tified increased serum TNF-α, IL-6, and IL-1β, which appeared 
to be associated with increased activation of both the NF-κB and 
mitogen-activated protein kinase (MAPK) signaling pathways in 
the thymus and spleen (Duan et al., 2017). In a rat microglial cell 
line, arsenic trioxide induced the secretion of inflammatory cytokine 
IL-6 by activating the Akt and NF-κB pathways (Chen et al., 2016). 
Studies with arsenic trioxide demonstrated improved immune 
targeting against liver cancer by increasing effector T cells and 
decreasing Tregs in the tumor. Additionally, in vitro studies dem-
onstrated an increase in the numbers of NKT, CD8+, and NK cells 
and in vitro cytotoxicity by arsenic trioxide (Wang et al., 2017b). In 
human PBMCs stimulated in vitro and exposed to arsenic disulfide, 
there was a decreased frequency of CD4+ and CD8+ T cells but 
the frequency of Tregs within the CD4+ cell population increased 
(Song et al., 2015). Arsenic increased the expression and secre-
tion of pro-inflammatory cytokines (IL-1α, IL-1β, and IL-18) in a 
human keratinocyte cell line, and the increase in IL-1β and IL-18 
secretion appears to be mediated by the induction of the inflamma-
some AIM2 (absent in melanoma 2) (Zhang et al., 2016b). In sum-
mary, arsenic alters inflammatory and adaptive immune processes 
but variations in the effects may be due to the speciation of arsenic 
and the target cell.
Mercury  Mercury compounds include organic (i.e., methyl mer-
cury) or inorganic forms. Mercury compounds suppress immuno-
logical responses, but also induce autoimmunity (see the section 
“Autoimmunity: Mercury”). Interestingly, in one genetically sus-
ceptible mouse model of autoimmunity, subcutaneous adminis-
tration of methyl mercury reduced T- and B-cell numbers prior to 
autoimmunity induction, demonstrating the immunomodulatory 
actions of mercury in vivo (Haggqvist et al., 2005). Similar to arse-
nic, the mechanism by which mercury compounds are immuno-
toxic might be through induction of apoptosis (Yole et al., 2007). 
Mercury also induces oxidative stress as evidenced by glutathione 
depletion and impaired mitochondrial function (Mondal et al., 
2005; Jorissen et al., 2013). Several studies have examined the 
effect of in vitro treatment of human PBMC with mercury com-
pounds. Mercuric chloride treatment of PBMCs stimulated with 
either α-CD3/CD28/CD40 or bacterial antigen demonstrated that 
mercury differentially affects Th1/Th2 cytokines, depending on the 
mode of T-cell activation (Hemdan et al., 2007). Mercuric chloride 
enhanced an LPS-induced inflammatory response in PBMCs and 
increased cytokine secretion from human monocyte-derived DCs 
(Gardner et al., 2009; Rachmawati et al., 2015). The induction of 
pro-inflammatory cytokines was also observed in Brazilian gold 
miners using mercury as an amalgam. Mercury-exposed miners

Toxic Responses of the Immune System
Chapter 12
681
demonstrated higher levels of IL-1β, TNF-α, and IFN-γ (Gardner 
et al., 2010). Increased pro-inflammatory cytokines were also 
identified in a Russian cohort occupationally exposed to mercury 
vapors; interestingly, there was an inverse relationship between 
serum cytokine concentrations and severity of neurological symp-
toms (Bodienkova et al., 2014). Additionally, environmental expo-
sure to low levels of mercury was associated with increased atopic 
dermatitis in adults and asthma in children in two Korean cohorts 
(Park and Kim, 2011; Kim et al., 2015).
The mechanism for cytokine induction could be due to 
­mercury-induced phosphorylation of ERK and p38 MAPKs, as 
demonstrated using mercuric chloride in a human T-cell line. This 
was due, in part, to ROS, because mercury-induced phosphoryla-
tion of ERK and p38 MAPKs was abrogated by N-acetyl cysteine 
(Haase et al., 2011). Similarly, treatment of a B-cell line with low 
levels of inorganic mercury induced tyrosine phosphorylation of 
proteins involved in the B-cell signaling pathway, whereas high 
levels induced cytotoxicity (Caruthers et al., 2014). Independent of 
an effect on MAPK signaling but dependent on ROS, organic and 
inorganic mercury induced the formation of extracellular traps by 
primary human neutrophils (neutrophil extracellular traps [NETs]) 
(Haase et al., 2016b). Therefore, depending on the concentration, 
mercury exposure could be cytotoxic or potentially inflammatory, 
and alter self-tolerance mechanisms.
Cadmium  Like other metals, cadmium exhibits immunomodu-
latory effects. Early studies demonstrated that oral adminis-
tration of cadmium to mice increased susceptibility to herpes 
simplex type 2 virus, suppressed T- and B-cell proliferation, but 
enhanced macrophage phagocytosis (Thomas et al., 1985b). As 
with many other immunotoxic agents, it has been suggested that 
the stress response, a shift to a Th2 cell population, induction of 
oxidative stress, and induction of apoptosis all contribute to the 
mechanism by which cadmium suppresses humoral immunity 
and CMI (Lall and Dan, 1999; Hemdan et al., 2006; Pathak and 
Khandelwal, 2006, 2008; Ebaid et al., 2014; ben Salah-Abbes 
et al., 2015; Milnerowicz et al., 2015). Interestingly, subchronic 
exposure to cadmium resulted in decreased inflammatory cyto-
kines and demonstrated a protective role in an animal model of 
colitis, which appears to contrast with studies demonstrating an 
increase in inflammation, and may reflect differences in dosing 
and experimental models (Blum et al., 2014; Milnerowicz et al., 
2015; Breton et al., 2016). Indeed in an animal model of arthritis, 
the inflammatory response and disease severity were decreased 
with lower doses of cadmium, but enhanced with higher doses of 
cadmium (Ansari et al., 2015).
A human study of workers occupationally and chronically 
exposed to cadmium identified an association between cadmium 
and lower percentages of neutrophils but higher percentages of 
lymphocytes (Ciarrocca et al., 2015). Using human HSCs isolated 
from umbilical cord blood, it was shown that in vitro treatment 
with cadmium chloride activated autophagy, an internal cellular 
degradation pathway allowing clearance of damaged cellular com-
ponents (Di Gioacchino et al., 2008). Cadmium-induced oxidative 
stress was confirmed in PBMC isolated from a Japanese popula-
tion of women living in a cadmium polluted area in which higher 
blood and urinary cadmium correlated with increased expression 
of several genes involved in oxidative stress signaling (Dakeshita 
et al., 2009). Furthermore, a cross-sectional analysis of data from 
NHANES found an association between blood cadmium levels and 
an increased risk of chronic infections (i.e., H. pylori and hepatitis 
B virus) (Krueger and Wade, 2016).
It has long been known that cadmium (and other metals, such 
as mercury) binds to a protein called metallothionein, which is a 
small, cysteine-rich protein that complexes normally with divalent 
cations, such as copper and zinc. The role of metallothionein in 
metal-induced immunotoxicity has been reviewed (Klaassen et al., 
2009) and there are several mechanisms by which the cadmium–
metallothionein complex might contribute to immune modulation. 
The binding of cadmium to metallothionein could displace copper 
or zinc, altering the availability of the latter cations for biochemical 
processes. Correspondingly, zinc treatment lessened the oxidative 
stress and the decreased lymphocyte survival induced by cadmium 
(Ebaid et al., 2014). Alternatively, metallothionein is induced in 
response to several stimuli, including metals, and it has been dem-
onstrated that metallothionein influences lymphocyte proliferation, 
differentiation, and various effector functions. Finally, because 
metallothionein is a cysteine-rich protein, it also plays a role in 
the oxidative homeostasis of the cell, which could be compromised 
under conditions of oxidative stress.
Solvents and Related Chemicals
There is limited but substantive evidence that exposure to organic 
solvents and their related compounds can produce immune sup-
pression. Chemicals included in this section are aromatic hydrocar-
bons, such as benzene, haloalkanes and haloalkenes, glycols and 
glycol ethers, and nitrosamines.
Aromatic Hydrocarbons  By far the best characterized immuno-
toxic effects by an organic solvent are those produced by benzene. 
In animal models, benzene induces anemia, lymphopenia, and 
hypoplastic bone marrow. In addition, it has been suggested that 
this myelotoxicity may be a result of altered differentiative capacity 
in bone marrow–derived lymphoid cells. Benzene (oral and inhaled) 
exposure has been reported to alter both humoral and CMI param-
eters, including suppression of the anti-sRBC antibody response, 
decreased T- and B-cell lymphoproliferative responses (mitogens 
and alloantigens), and suppression of CTL activity. Pyatt et al. 
(1998) demonstrated that hydroquinone (HQ), a reactive metabo-
lite of benzene, inhibits the activity of NF-κB. This HQ-induced 
inhibition of NF-κB has also been reported in monocytic cells and 
likely accounts for the HQ-induced inhibition of IL-12 in macro-
phages (Kim et al., 2005). The HQ-induced inhibition of IL-12, 
coupled with an HQ-induced increase in IL-4 in CD4+ T cells (Lee 
et al., 2002), suggests that part of the mechanism by which ben-
zene alters immune function involves metabolic activation to HQ, 
which promotes a Th2 phenotype. HQ induces apoptosis, which 
might also contribute to its reported decreases in human neutro-
phils, eosinophils (Yang et al., 2011), and naive CD4+ T cells (Li 
et al., 2009). Interestingly, in chronic benzene-exposed work-
ers, there is a decrease in CD4+ cells as well as lower expression 
of genes encoding for the CD3 complex proteins, CD3γ, CD3δ, 
CD3ε, and CD3ξ (Li et al., 2012), which likely contributes to com-
promised T-cell responsiveness.
A number of compounds structurally related to benzene have 
also been studied for their potential effects on the immune system. 
For example, nitrobenzene (an oxidizing agent used in the synthesis 
of aniline and benzene compounds) has been reported to produce 
immunotoxic effects (Burns et al., 1994b), with the primary targets 
being erythrocytes and bone marrow. Immunomodulating activity 
has also been observed for toluene, although most effects occur 
at markedly high concentrations. When compared with benzene, 
toluene has little to no effect on immunocompetence. In contrast 
to the parent toluene, the monosubstituted nitrotoluenes (para- and 
meta-nitrotoluene) do significantly alter the immune system (Burns 
et al., 1994a, 1994c). Exposure to p-nitrotoluene suppresses the 
antibody response to sRBC, decreases the number of CD4+ splenic

682
Target Organ Toxicity
Unit IV
T cells, and suppresses the DTH response to KLH. In addition, host 
resistance to L. monocytogenes is impaired, suggesting the T cell 
as a primary target. Similarly, m-nitrotoluene suppresses the anti-
body response to sRBC, the DTH response to KLH, T-cell mitogen-
esis, and host resistance to L. monocytogenes, again suggesting the 
T cell as the cellular target. The di-substituted nitrotoluene (2,4- 
dinitrotoluene) is also immunosuppressive (Burns et al., 1994a), 
with exposure resulting in suppressed humoral immunity, NK cell 
activity, and phagocytosis by splenic macrophages.
Haloalkanes and Haloalkenes  Carbon tetrachloride is widely 
recognized as hepatotoxic. Studies in mice revealed that carbon tet-
rachloride is also immunosuppressive. Mice exposed for 7 to 30 
days to carbon tetrachloride (orally or intraperitoneally) exhibit a 
decreased TDAR (sRBC), suppressed MLR, and lower lympho-
proliferative capacity (T and B cells) (Kaminski et al., 1989a). 
Ex vivo activation of splenic T cells isolated from carbon tetrachlo-
ride–treated mice reveal a marked enhancement in IL-2 production. 
Moreover the effect on IL-2 by carbon tetrachloride is associ-
ated with a serum factor in treated animals as direct addition of 
serum from carbon tetrachloride treated mice also produced strong 
enhancement of IL-2 in naive activated splenic T cells. Further 
characterization demonstrated that exposure to carbon tetrachloride 
causes the induction and release of TGF-β1 from the liver, which 
is concomitant with the onset of immune suppression. Addition of 
anti-TGF-β1 neutralizing antibodies abrogate IL-2 enhancement by 
serum isolated from carbon tetrachloride-treated mice, suggesting 
that the enhancing effects on IL-2 are mediated indirectly through 
TGF-β1 released from the liver (Delaney et al., 1994; Jeon et al., 
1997). Induction or inhibition of liver cytochrome P-450 activity 
augment and block, respectively, the immunotoxic actions of car-
bon tetrachloride, suggesting a requirement for metabolism in order 
for carbon tetrachloride to be immunosuppressive (Kaminski et al., 
1990). Subsequently, it was demonstrated that TGF-β1 produces 
profound effects on IL-2 regulation. Specifically, results in mouse 
spleen cells showed that TGF-β1 exerts bifunctional effects on 
IL-2 and lymphoproliferation as evidenced by the fact that TGF-β1 
stimulates IL-2 production at low concentrations (0.1 to 1 pg/mL) 
and conversely suppresses IL-2 production at high concentrations 
(1 to 10 ng/mL), when activated using anti-CD3/CD28 (McKarns 
and Kaminski, 2000). Additionally, concentrations of TGF-β1 that 
stimulate IL-2 production concomitantly suppress splenocyte pro-
liferation under similar conditions. More recent studies have iden-
tified prostaglandin E2 (PGE2) as another soluble factor released 
following carbon tetrachloride liver injury (O’Brien et al., 2014). 
It was demonstrated that expression of cyclooxygenase-2 (COX-2), 
the enzyme that drives synthesis of PGE2, is upregulated in both 
liver and lung in carbon tetrachloride–treated mice. Plasma from 
carbon tetrachloride–treated mice suppresses TNF-α production in 
macrophages and this is not observed if the plasma was isolated 
from carbon tetrachloride–treated mice that had also received the 
nonselective COX inhibitor, indomethacin (O’Brien et al., 2014). 
Together these results suggest that the mechanism for compro-
mised immunity in response to carbon tetrachloride is due, in part, 
to release of immune suppressive mediators, including TGF-β1 and 
PGE2, from the liver.
There is relatively little information on solvents and other 
chemicals structurally related to carbon tetrachloride. Some early 
studies were conducted to assess the potential immunotoxicity of 
a number of drinking water contaminants. Exposure to dichloro-
ethylene (in drinking water for 90 days) suppresses the anti-sRBC 
antibody response in male CD-1 mice and inhibits macrophage 
function in females (Shopp et al., 1985). Similarly, exposure to tri-
chloroethylene (in the drinking water for 4 to 6 months) inhibits 
both humoral immunity and CMI and bone marrow colony-form-
ing activity (Sanders et al., 1982). In those experiments, females 
were more sensitive than males. Exposure to 1,1,2-trichloroeth-
ane resulted in suppression of humoral immunity in both sexes. In 
addition, macrophage function is suppressed (males only) (Sanders 
et al., 1985). Inhalation studies with dichloroethane, dichlorometh-
ane, tetrachloroethane, and trichloroethene indicate that the pulmo-
nary host resistance to Klebsiella pneumoniae is suppressed (Aranyi 
et al., 1986; Sherwood et al., 1987), suggesting that alveolar mac-
rophages might be affected. More recent work with this series of 
solvents indicate that exposure to trichloroethylene might be an 
effective developmental immunotoxicant in B6C3F1 mice, suggest-
ing that additional studies are required to determine the health risks 
associated with developmental exposure to this chemical (Peden-
Adams et al., 2006).
Nitrosamines  The nitrosamine family of chemicals com-
prises the nitrosamines, nitrosamides, and C-nitroso compounds. 
Exposure to nitrosamines, especially N-nitrosodimethylamine 
(e.g., also known as dimethylnitrosamine [DMN], and the most 
prevalent nitrosamine) comes primarily through industrial and 
dietary means, and minimally through environmental exposure. 
DMN is used commonly as an industrial solvent in the production 
of dimethylhydrazine. It has been used as an antioxidant, as an addi-
tive for lubricants and gasolines, and as a softener of copolymers. 
The toxicity and immunotoxicity of DMN have been extensively 
reviewed (Myers and Schook, 1996). Single or repeated exposure 
to DMN inhibits T-dependent humoral immune responses (IgM 
and IgG), but not T-independent responses. Other symmetrical 
nitrosamines, such as diethylnitrosamine (DEN), dipropylnitro-
samine, and dibutylnitrosamine, demonstrated similar effects on 
humoral immunity but are not as potent as DMN (Kaminski et al., 
1989b). T-cell-mediated lymphoproliferative responses (mitogens 
or MLR) and DTH response are also suppressed following DMN 
exposure. DMN induces apoptosis in human leukocytes, which 
could account for some of the compromised immune responses 
(Iwaniuk et al., 2015). In vivo exposure to DMN in mice followed 
by challenge with several pathogens does not produce a consistent 
pattern of effects: decreased resistance to Streptococcus zooepi-
demicus and influenza, no effects on resistance to herpes simplex 
types 1 or 2 or Trichinella spiralis, and increased resistance to 
L. monocytogenes. In contrast, anti-tumor activity in DMN-
exposed animals is consistently enhanced (Thomas et al., 1985a). 
This could be related to enhanced macrophage activity, increased 
myelopoietic activity, and alterations in TNF-α transcriptional 
activity by DMN. DMN is also a potent liver carcinogen because it 
is metabolized by cytochrome P450s to a strong alkylating agent. 
There have been reports that DEN-induced hepatocarcinogenesis 
induces inflammatory responses (Schneider et al., 2012), which 
might explain enhanced macrophage activity following exposure 
to DMN.
Mycotoxins
Mycotoxins are structurally diverse secondary metabolites of fungi 
that grow on feed. This class of chemicals comprises such toxins 
as aflatoxin, ochratoxin, and the tricothecenes, notably T-2 toxin 
and deoxynivalenol (vomitoxin). As a class, these toxins can pro-
duce immune stimulation or immune suppression depending on 
dose, exposure frequency, or route of administration (Pestka et al., 
2004). For the extensively studied trichothecenes, the mechanism 
of immune impairment is related in part to inhibition of protein 
synthesis. The mechanism for trichothecene-induced immune sup-
pression at high doses also involves leukocyte apoptosis (Pestka

Toxic Responses of the Immune System
Chapter 12
683
et al., 1994). Conversely, at low doses, trichothecenes promote 
expression of a diverse array of cytokines including IL-1, IL-2, 
IL-5, and IL-6. In addition, trichothecenes activate MAPKs in vivo 
and in vitro via a mechanism known as the ribotoxic stress response 
(Moon and Pestka, 2002; Chung et al., 2003; Zhou et al., 2003; Pan 
et al., 2013). Detailed studies in Jurkat cells have identified that 
the ribotoxic stress response to deoxynivalenol involves increased 
intracellular calcium (Katika et al., 2015). Prolonged consump-
tion of deoxynivalenol by mice induces elevation of IgA and IgA 
immune complex formation, and kidney mesangial IgA deposition 
(Pestka, 2003). It has been postulated that the enhancement in IgA 
production induced by deoxynivalenol might be associated with 
the increase in cytokine production described above. The tricho-
thecenes are currently considered among the most potent small-
molecule inhibitors of protein synthesis in eukaryotic cells, which 
is dichotomous to the observed increase in IgA secretion.
Past studies with aflatoxin B1 suggest that CMI and phagocytic 
cell functions are affected as evidenced by decreased proliferative 
responses to PHA and suppression of DTH responses (Raisuddin 
et al., 1993). In addition, in vitro experiments demonstrate that 
aflatoxin B1 requires metabolic bioactivation in order to produce 
suppression of antibody responses and of mitogen-induced lympho-
proliferation (Yang et al., 1986). Studies in laboratory animals have 
also shown increased risk to secondary infection after aflatoxin B1 
treatment. The effects of aflatoxins on the human immune system 
have not been extensively characterized, but there is an association 
between high exposure to aflatoxins and lower T- and B-cell counts 
(Jiang et al., 2005).
Adverse health effects have been associated with damp indoor 
environments following building envelope breech resulting from 
heavy rains and/or flooding, as occurred during Hurricanes Katrina 
and Rita in the Gulf Coast of the United States. The adverse health 
effects have been attributed, at least in part, to the presence of 
molds, most notably Stachybotrys chartarum, also known as black 
mold. Stachybotrys chartarum produces the macrocyclic trichot-
hecene toxin, satratoxin G, which like many of the trichothecenes 
is a potent inhibitor of protein synthesis. In one study, satratoxin G 
exposure of mice, 100 µg/kg for 5 consecutive days by intranasal 
instillation, induces apoptosis of olfactory sensory neurons and neu-
trophilic rhinitis (Islam et al., 2006). Elevated mRNA levels for pro 
-inflammatory cytokines TNF-α, IL-6, and IL-1, and the chemo-
kine, MIP-2, are detected in nasal airways and the adjacent olfac-
tory bulb of the brain. By day 7, marked atrophy of the olfactory 
nerve and glomerular layer of the olfactory bulb is detected. An 
additional study also showed that satratoxin G exposure produces 
epithelial injury and olfactory sensory neuron apoptosis in rhesus 
monkeys (Carey et al., 2012). These findings suggest that neu-
rotoxicity and inflammation within the nose might be potential 
adverse health effects associated with Stachybotrys exposure in 
indoor air.
Natural and Synthetic Hormones
It is well established that a sexual dimorphism exists in the immune 
system. Females have higher levels of circulating Igs, a greater 
antibody response, and a higher incidence of autoimmune disease 
than do males. Males appear to be more susceptible to the develop-
ment of sepsis and the mortality associated with soft tissue trauma 
and hemorrhagic shock. Specific natural sex hormones have been 
implicated to mediate this dichotomy with the evolutionary goal 
of increased mating success and species propagation (reviewed by 
Foo et al. [2017] and  Roved et al. [2017]). A recent cross-species 
meta-analysis of immune function supports a general immunosup-
pressive effect of testosterone versus an immune-enhancing effect 
of estrogen on humoral-mediated immune function and cytokine 
levels, but estrogens also induced an immunosuppressive effect on 
cell-mediated immune function (Foo et al., 2017). Further analysis 
supports a decrease in Th2 and Th17 immune responses, a variable 
effect on Th1 immune responses, and increased wound healing by 
testosterone, whereas estrogen enhances Th2 immune responses 
and decreases Th1 and Th17 immune responses (reviewed by 
Roved et al. [2017]). Physiologically, immune effects of sex hor-
mones appear to be very tightly controlled, and profound changes 
in immune activity can result for very slight changes in concentra-
tions of hormones.
Estrogens  Diethylstilbestrol is a synthetic nonsteroidal com-
pound possessing estrogenic activity. Diethylstilbestrol was used in 
men to treat prostate cancer and in women as an estrogen replace-
ment or contraceptive drug, and to prevent threatened abortions; 
however, use during pregnancy was later associated with develop-
ment of genital adenocarcinoma in female offspring at the onset of 
puberty (Herbst et al., 1971). Extensive functional and host resis-
tance studies on diethylstilbestrol (mg/kg/day dose range) have 
indicated that exposure to this chemical results in alterations in 
CMI and/or macrophage function that are thought to be mediated 
by the presence of the estrogen receptor in immune cells (Kalland, 
1980; Luster et al., 1980, 1984; Holsapple et al., 1983; Lai et al., 
2000; Brown et al., 2006a, 2006b). Targeted sites of action include 
the thymus (thymic depletion and alteration in T-cell maturation 
process), T cells (decreased MLR, DTH, and lymphoproliferative 
responses), and macrophages (enhanced phagocytic, anti-tumor, 
and suppressor function). Pre- and neonatal exposures (mg/kg/
day dose range) also demonstrate immunotoxic effects related to 
T cell dysfunction. DTH and inflammatory responses associated 
with diethylstilbestrol exposure in adult mice have been shown 
to be reversible upon cessation of exposure (Luster et al., 1980; 
Holsapple et al., 1983). However, effects from in utero and neo-
natal exposures appear to have more lasting, possibly permanent 
effects on immune responses (Kalland et al., 1979; Luster et al., 
1979; Ways et al., 1980).
Exposure of human PBMC from men and women to 
17β-estradiol (E2) increases basal IgM, IgG, and IL-10 production 
with no effect on IL-1α, IL-1β, IL-2, IL-4, and IL-6. Interestingly, 
addition of IL10 enhances the effect of E2 on IgM and IgG pro-
duction (Kanda et al., 1999). IL-10 appears to have both positive 
and negative effects on immune function, because in mouse models 
IL-10 is the mediator of a Breg subset (i.e., B10 or Br1). Similar 
to the above results, E2 enhances the induction of IgM and IgA 
AFCs from PBMC stimulated with pokeweed mitogen (testosterone 
had no effect) (Paavonen et al., 1981). In an autoimmune disease 
state, basal IgG production is more markedly induced by E2 in 
patients with active systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE) as com-
pared to normal donors. In addition to an increase in total IgG, E2 
also induces anti-dsDNA antibody (absent in normal donors). The 
profile is somewhat different for inactive SLE patients in that E2 
increased total IgG production to a similar degree as for active SLE 
patients. Moreover, E2 does not induce anti-dsDNA antibody, which 
has a lower basal level in inactive SLE than active SLE (Kanda 
et al., 1999). Furthermore, microarray analysis of T cells purified 
from human PBMC demonstrated E2-altered gene expression for 
cellular signaling proteins in activated T cells from SLE patients 
versus controls (Walters et al., 2009). In endometriosis, estrogen 
levels, Tregs, and IL-10 are higher. Estrogen appears to induce 
IDO1 expression leading to increased Tregs and IL-10 levels as 
well as increase survival and invasion of endometrial stromal cells, 
perhaps by enhanced immune tolerance to these cells via Tregs 
(Wei et al., 2016). Interestingly, in an experimental mouse model of

684
Target Organ Toxicity
Unit IV
drug-induced liver injury, E2 decreases Tregs and exacerbates hepatic 
injury, which is dependent on IL-6 (Cho et al., 2013). E2 therapy 
(1 mg) for 14 to 17 weeks in postmenopausal women has no effect 
on serum Ig levels or IL-4, IL-6, IL-10, and IL-7 levels compared 
to those before E2 therapy (Itaborahy and de Medeiros, 2016), but 
a review of studies evaluating immune response in postmenopausal 
women following hormone replacement therapy suggests immuno-
logical changes; the most consistent being a decrease in IL-6 levels 
(Abdi et al., 2016). ERβ activation in CD4+ T cells is anti-inflamma-
tory (induces Treg and inhibits Th17 polarization) in PBMCs from 
multiple sclerosis (MS) patients and in an EAE mouse model, as 
well as neuroprotective in the EAE mouse model (Aggelakopoulou 
et al., 2016).
Isoflavones (“isoflavone intervention”) administered for 16 
weeks to postmenopausal women through soymilk or supplemental 
tablets results in higher frequency of B cells in peripheral blood 
with no effect on the frequency of CD4+, CD8+, or NK subsets. 
The isoflavone intervention has no effect on IL-2 or TNF-α plasma 
levels, but IFN-γ trended toward an almost twofold nonsignificant 
increase (Ryan-Borchers et al., 2006). Other studies demonstrated 
an increase in plasma IL-6 with isoflavone diets (73 mg/day) 
(Jenkins et al., 2002) and increased cytotoxicity of human PBMC 
NK cells with isoflavone metabolites (0.1 μmol/L) (Zhang et al., 
1999). In contrast to the human results with E2, in vivo administra-
tion of the phytoestrogen genistein suppresses anti-KLH IgG titers 
in mice (Yellayi et al., 2002). Genistein also inhibits cell-mediated 
immunity, but in an ER-independent manner. However, mouse cells 
treated with E2, diethylstilbestrol, or bisphenol A (discussed below) 
enhance IgG anti-DNA antibody production and IgG immune com-
plex deposition in the kidney, which may be a result of increased 
autoantibody secretion from B-1 B cells (Yurino et al., 2004). 
Additionally, E2 has been shown to drive the expansion of the 
mouse Treg compartment and to increase Treg activity (Polanczyk 
et al., 2004; Luo et al., 2011). E2 also inhibits recruitment and acti-
vation of inflammatory cells resulting in inhibition of TNF-α and 
IFN-γ production and of the inflammatory response (Salem et al., 
2000). These discrepancies in the effects of E2 (activation vs. inhi-
bition) may be due to species differences, different cellular targets, 
or a lack of disease state. Taken together it is clear that E2 and 
phytoestrogens alter the human antibody response, an effect that is 
more marked in an autoimmune disease state.
A potential mechanism for the effects by estrogen and estro-
genic compounds is activation of the nuclear estrogen receptor 
(ER, α or β isoform), which is ubiquitously expressed in most tis-
sues. Binding of ligand stabilizes ER dimers, which then bind to 
estrogen response elements (ERE) in target genes, leading to tran-
scriptional activation or inhibition. In-depth reviews outline studies 
evaluating ER expression and ER-mediated effects in innate and 
adaptive immunity from human and animal models as well as a 
role of ER in autoimmunity (Cunningham and Gilkeson, 2011; 
Kovats, 2015; Khan and Ahmed, 2016). In resting and activated 
human PBMC, CD4+ T cells express higher levels of ERα than 
B cells, which express higher levels of ERβ. CD8+ cells express 
ERα and ERβ equally, but at low levels (Phiel et al., 2005). ERα 
and ERβ are differentially expressed in APCs (Cunningham and 
Gilkeson, 2011) and in human secondary lymphoid tissues (Shim 
et al., 2006). Additionally, estrogen and the ER appear to play a 
significant role in autoimmunity. Ovariectomy before sexual matu-
ration results in a significant suppression in a murine model of SLE, 
which can be reversed by supplementary estrogen treatment (Talal, 
1981). ER knockout studies have primarily corroborated this find-
ing with knockout of ERα in three different mouse models of SLE 
showing attenuation of the disease and prolonged survival (Bynote 
et al., 2008; Svenson et al., 2008). This effect is selective for ERα, 
because ERβ does not alter the disease state (Svenson et al., 2008). 
In contrast to these results, ERα (but not ERβ) knockout in a mildly 
autoimmune prone mouse strain leads to signs of autoimmunity and 
spontaneous (no antigen challenge) formation of germinal centers 
in the spleen (Shim et al., 2004). Indeed, unlike SLE, both ERα and 
ERβ exhibit a protective effect in MS and its experimental model 
(EAE), which may reflect differing functions of these receptors 
in different tissues and cell types (reviewed by Khan and Ahmed 
[2016]). An additional consideration in elucidating the mechanisms 
by which the ER mediates biological effects is the extensive cross-
talk that can occur between the ER and other receptor signaling 
pathways (e.g., AhR and PPAR) and transcription factors (e.g., 
AP-1 and NF-κB) that may be independent of the ERE (Bonofiglio 
et al., 2005; Marino et al., 2006; Matthews and Gustafsson, 2006).
Bisphenol A, a monomer in polycarbonate plastics and a con-
stituent of epoxy and polystyrene resins possessing weak binding 
affinity for the estrogen receptor, has been recently evaluated by 
a number of laboratories for its potential to affect various aspects 
of immune function (reviewed by Kharrazian [2014]). The major-
ity of studies to date demonstrate that leukocytes cultured in the 
presence of very high concentrations (more than 1 µM) of bisphe-
nol A exhibit a number of alterations primarily in innate immune 
function responses including suppression of LPS-induced nitric 
oxide production and TNF-α secretion by macrophages (Kim and 
Jeong, 2003). The effects on nitric oxide production correlate with 
a decrease in NF-κB DNA binding activity, a transcription factor 
critically involved in the regulation of inducible nitric oxide syn-
thase and TNF-α. In this study, the estrogen receptor antagonist, 
ICI 182,780, blocks bisphenol A suppression of LPS-induced nitric 
oxide production. Bisphenol A (10 to 50 µM) has also been reported 
to enhance IL-4 production in a model of a secondary immune 
response (Lee et al., 2003). In vivo treatment of mice with bisphe-
nol A (2.5 mg/kg) for 7 days produces a decrease in ex vivo Con 
A–induced proliferation and IFN-γ secretion, but has no effect on 
the number of CD4+, CD8+, and CD19+ cells in the spleen (Sawai 
et al., 2003). Additional studies corroborate these findings by dem-
onstrating an augmentation of Th1 immune responses (i.e., cytokine 
profile and increased expression of antigen-specific IgG2a and IgA) 
with one study showing increased Th1 and Th2 immune responses. 
In these studies, bisphenol A was administered either by i.p. injec-
tion (0.1 mg/g) four times every second day (Alizadeh et al., 2006), 
in drinking water (10 mg/L, ad libitum) for 2 weeks (Goto et al., 
2007) or through maternal dosing (3 to 3000 μg/kg) for 18 days 
(Yoshino et al., 2004). Presently, the putative effects of bisphenol A 
on immune function are poorly defined and based on the current lit-
erature; it is unclear whether the majority of the immunomodulatory 
effects reported are mediated through an ER-dependent mechanism. 
Additionally, the relatively high potential for human exposure to 
bisphenol A due to its wide use in plastics and other products has 
been of considerable concern to the public and government regula-
tors. Despite the large number of safety-related studies published 
on bisphenol A, there is considerable controversy regarding its 
safety and the current tolerable daily intake value (0.05 mg/kg body 
weight/day) (Sekizawa, 2008; Vandenberg et al., 2009; Hengstler 
et al., 2011). Therefore, the National Toxicology Program, National 
Institute of Environmental Health Sciences, and U.S. Food and Drug 
Administration jointly developed a consortium-based research pro-
gram known as the Consortium Linking Academic and Regulatory 
Insights on BPA Toxicity (CLARITY-BPA) to conduct hypothesis-
driven research to address this controversy (Heindel et al., 2015). 
Results from the immunotoxicological evaluation of BPA from the 
CLARITY study are forthcoming.

Toxic Responses of the Immune System
Chapter 12
685
Androgens  Oxymetholone is a synthetic androgen structurally 
related to testosterone and was used in the past in the treatment 
of pituitary dwarfism and as an adjunctive therapy in osteopo-
rosis. Its current use is limited to treatment of certain anemias. 
Oxymetholone administered orally to male mice daily for 14 con-
secutive days (50 to 300 mg/kg/day) (Karrow et al., 2000) results in 
a minimal decrease in CMI (MLR and CTL response) without alter-
ing the ability of the animals to resist infection in host resistance 
assays. In contrast, anabolic androgenic steroids have been shown 
to significantly suppress the sRBC AFC response and to increase 
the production of pro-inflammatory cytokines from human PBMC.
Testosterone through the activation of the cytosolic andro-
gen receptor (AR) is capable of contributing to the suppression of 
immune function, particularly, macrophage activity, CMI responses, 
and the development of T and B cells (reviewed by Lai et al. [2012] 
and Trigunaite et al. [2015]). There are numerous reports in the 
clinical literature that males are more susceptible than females 
to infection following soft tissue trauma and hemorrhagic shock 
(reviewed by Catania and Chaudry [1999]). Treatment of males 
with chemicals that block testosterone (e.g., flutamide) can pre-
vent the trauma- and hemorrhage-induced depression of immunity. 
Similarly, treatment of females with dihydrotestosterone prior to 
trauma hemorrhage results in depression of CMI similar to that of 
males. Furthermore, gonadectomized mice of either sex have ele-
vated immune responses to endotoxin, which can be attenuated in 
either sex by the administration of testosterone. Studies with gen-
eral and conditional AR knockout models underscore the role of 
the AR in regulating immune function, including supporting the 
generation and activation of neutrophils and the pro-inflammatory 
responses of macrophages but suppressing lymphocyte develop-
ment and adaptive immune responses (reviewed by Lai et al. [2012] 
and Trigunaite et al. [2015]).
Glucocorticoids  The immunosuppressive actions of corticoste-
roids have been known for years (reviewed by Oppong and Cato 
[2015]). Following binding to a cytosolic receptor, these chemi-
cals produce profound lymphoid cell depletion in rodent models. 
In NHPs and humans, lymphopenia associated with decreased 
monocytes and eosinophils and increased neutrophils are seen. 
Corticosteroids induce apoptosis in rodents; T cells and B cells 
(pro-B to mature B-cell maturation states) are particularly sensitive 
(Gruver-Yates et al., 2014). In addition, these chemicals suppress 
macrophage function, the production of IL-1 from the macro-
phages, and the subsequent synthesis of IL-2 by T cells. In general, 
corticosteroids suppress the generation of CTL responses, MLR, 
NK cell activity, and lymphoproliferation. Glucocorticoids appear 
to induce DCs to promote the development of Tregs, favor a Th1 to 
Th2 shift, and induce the AFC response and secretion of IgE and 
IgG4. Increased IgE seems contrary to the use of glucocorticoids 
in allergy; however, these drugs have a strongly inhibitory effect 
on eosinophils and mast cells and appear to reduce antigen-specific 
IgE (reviewed by Franchimont [2004]).
In spite of the wide therapeutic use of glucocorticoids, the 
mechanism of action by which glucocorticoids mediate their anti-
inflammatory/immunosuppressive activity is not well understood. 
Several mechanisms have been proposed, all of which involve acti-
vation of the glucocorticoid receptor (GR) (reviewed by Baschant 
and Tuckermann [2010]). Binding of glucocorticoids to the cyto-
solic GR (member of the nuclear receptor superfamily) activates 
the receptor to function as a ligand-activated transcription factor 
that undergoes homodimerization and DNA binding to glucocor-
ticoid response elements (GRE) in the regulatory regions of glu-
cocorticoid responsive genes. Depending on the gene, GRE can 
either positively or negatively regulate transcription. For example, 
glucocorticoids induce annexin 1 (lipocortin 1), a calcium and phos-
pholipid binding protein, which acts to inhibit PLA2 (Goulding and 
Guyre, 1992; Taylor et al., 1997; Perretti and D’Acquisto, 2009). 
Inhibition of PLA2 results in a decrease in arachidonate formation, 
the precursor in the biosynthesis of inflammatory prostaglandins 
and leukotrienes. Similarly, GR activation induces transcription 
of IκB, which is the endogenous inhibitor of the transcription fac-
tor NF-κB  (Auphan et al., 1995; Scheinman et al., 1995; Deroo 
and Archer, 2001). Because transcription of many key inflamma-
tory cytokines is regulated positively by NF-κB, induction of IκB 
results in retention of NF-κB in the cytosol and thus suppression of 
inflammatory cytokine production. Ligand-activated GRs have also 
been found to physically interact with other transcription factors 
including AP-1 (Schule et al., 1990; Karmakar et al., 2013) and 
NF-κB (Ray and Prefontaine, 1994; Widen et al., 2003) to inhibit 
DNA binding and/or transcriptional activity. Alternatively, GRs can 
cooperatively interact with NF-κB in a negative feedback loop to 
induce the expression of anti-inflammatory genes (Altonsy et al., 
2014). Cross-talk between the GR and other nuclear receptors may 
also play a role in mediating the effects of glucocorticoid ligands 
(Karmakar et al., 2013). Presently, it is thought that all of the above 
mechanisms contribute to the anti-inflammatory and immunosup-
pressive properties of glucocorticoids.
Drugs of Abuse
Drug abuse is a social issue with extensive pathophysiological 
effects on the abuser. Drugs of abuse exhibit immunosuppres-
sive actions, and in fact it has been suggested that in addition to 
the direct risk of HIV contraction via needle sharing or judgment 
lapses, abuse of some drugs has been associated with disease pro-
gression to AIDS (Rogers, 2011). Several classes of drugs will be 
discussed, including cannabinoids, opioids, cocaine, methamphet-
amine, and ethanol. Reports regarding the immune system effects 
of many of these drugs are often contradictory, so it should be noted 
that the mechanisms by which drugs of abuse suppress immune 
function might depend on the development of tolerance or addic-
tion to the drugs; the immune, withdrawal, and pain status of the 
individual; and levels of endogenous molecules (i.e., endorphins or 
endocannabinoids).
Cannabinoids  Much attention has been focused on the immu-
nomodulatory effects of the cannabinoids, which can be defined as 
plant-derived (i.e., from the marijuana plant), synthetic, or endog-
enous. Therapeutically, the primary psychoactive congener of mari-
juana, ∆9-tetrahydrocannabinol, is federally approved in the United 
States in pill form as an antiemetic in patients undergoing cancer 
chemotherapy, and as an appetite stimulant for cachexia associated 
with advanced AIDS disease. Several states have also legalized 
marijuana for medical use over the last decade (Wilkinson et al., 
2016), which is smoked in joints or bongs, ingested in brownies, 
cookies, or teas, or vaporized.
The mechanism by which ∆9-tetrahydrocannabinol produces 
psychotropic effects is through a G protein–coupled cannabinoid 
receptor CB1 (Varvel et al., 2005). Peripheral tissues also express 
CB1, in addition to a second cannabinoid receptor CB2. Although 
both CB1 and CB2 are expressed on immune system cells (Galiegue 
et al., 1995), not all cannabinoid effects are mediated via the recep-
tors. For instance, in T cells, many plant-derived compounds sup-
press IL-2 in a CB1/CB2-independent manner (Kaplan et al., 2003). 
IL-2 suppression by ∆9-tetrahydrocannabinol is due, in part, to sup-
pression of transcription factor activation (AP-1, NFAT, NF-κB) 
and ERK MAPK activity (Condie et al., 1996; Herring et al., 1998;

686
Target Organ Toxicity
Unit IV
Faubert and Kaminski, 2000). In contrast, B-cell suppression by 
∆9-tetrahydrocannabinol is CB1 and/or CB2 receptor-dependent 
(Schatz et al., 1993; Springs et al., 2008). This is consistent with 
several studies identifying a critical role for CB2 in germinal cen-
ters, memory B cells, and T-cell-independent responses (Basu 
et al., 2011, 2013).
Cannabinoid suppression of other APCs, such as macrophages 
and DCs, is also mediated through the CB1 and/or CB2 receptors. 
∆9-Tetrahydrocannabinol exposure impairs lysosomal or cytochrome 
c processing in macrophages (McCoy et al., 1995; Matveyeva 
et al., 2000) likely via the CB2 receptor (Buckley et al., 2000; 
Chuchawankul et al., 2004). IL-12 production from DC and sub-
sequent Th1 activation are suppressed by ∆9-tetrahydrocannabinol 
in a CB1 and/or CB2 receptor-dependent manner (Lu et al., 2006a, 
2006b). Overall, several mechanisms are involved in cannabinoid-
mediated suppression of host resistance, including dysregulation of 
cytokine profiles (shifts in Th1/Th2/Th17 balance) (Newton et al., 
2009; Sido et al., 2016), induction of apoptosis (McKallip et al., 
2002), and iTregs (Rao et al., 2015; Dhital et al., 2017).
Opioids  Similar to cannabinoids, opioids refer to plant-derived, 
synthetic, or endogenous (endorphins) compounds that bind opioid 
receptors. Although technically “opioid” refers to drugs derived 
from the poppy plant, and “opiate” refers to agonists and antag-
onists with morphine-like activity (including plant-derived and 
synthetic compounds), they are often used interchangeably. It is 
well established that opioids suppress immune responses and the 
mechanism often involves one of the Gi-coupled opioid receptors 
(µ, κ, and δ receptors), but there are opioid receptor–independent 
mechanisms as well (Roy et al., 2011).
Early studies evaluating the immune competence of heroin 
addicts revealed a decrease in total T cells, which was reversed 
with the general opioid receptor antagonist naloxone, suggesting 
a role for an opioid receptor in mediating immune suppression 
(McDonough et al., 1980). Later studies demonstrated that some 
of the effects of morphine were the result of increased circulat-
ing corticosteroids (Pruett et al., 1992b; LeVier et al., 1994). Both 
mechanisms can contribute to decreased host resistance to viral and 
bacterial infection by opioids.
For instance, morphine treatment of mice infected with S. 
pneumoniae increases bacterial burden in the lungs and increased 
mortality. The mechanism for compromised host resistance involves 
suppression of NF-κB gene transcription, which likely contrib-
utes to decreased expression of inflammatory mediators, such as 
chemokines, reducing recruitment of neutrophils to the infection 
site (Wang et al., 2005). Morphine also reactivates latent herpes 
simplex virus infection due to suppressed CTL activity and IFN-γ 
production (Mojadadi et al., 2009). Morphine suppression of cyto-
kines, such as IL-2 and IFN-γ (Mojadadi et al., 2009), involves 
opioid-mediated suppression of many transcription factors, includ-
ing NF-κB, AP-1, and CREB (Roy et al., 1997; Wang et al., 2003, 
2005; Borner and Kraus, 2013).
Opioids also modulate innate immunity. NK cells are sensi-
tive targets of suppression by opioids (Saurer et al., 2004, 2006). 
Interestingly, the NK effects might be centrally mediated as cen-
tral administration of an opioid antagonist attenuates suppres-
sion by morphine (Shavit et al., 1986). Some studies demonstrate 
either suppression (Sacerdote, 2003) or enhancement (Peng et al., 
2000) of cytokine production from macrophages. The differences 
might be due to the agonist used, in vitro versus in vivo admin-
istration, and the dosing regimen (i.e., whether tolerance was 
induced or not). Overall, it is clear that opioids suppress immune 
function and that the mechanism by which this occurs is complex 
and likely involves the CNS, the autonomic nervous system, the 
hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis, and one or more opioid recep-
tors (Alonzo and Bayer, 2002).
Cocaine  Cocaine is a potent local anesthetic and CNS stimulant. 
This drug and its derivatives have been shown to alter several mea-
sures of immune competence, including humoral and cell-medi-
ated immune responses and host resistance (Watson et al., 1983; 
Ou et al., 1989; Starec et al., 1991). Studies evaluating the effect 
of acute in vivo cocaine exposure on the generation of the anti-
sRBC AFC determined that sex, strain, and age differences dic-
tate differences in cocaine-induced immunomodulation (Matulka 
et al., 1996), and that immune suppression is due to a cytochrome 
P-450 metabolite of cocaine (Jeong et al., 1996). Similar to other 
immunotoxic chemicals, the mechanism by which cocaine alters 
immune function involves a disruption of the Th1/Th2 balance 
and the stress response (Stanulis et al., 1997a, 1997b; Jankowski 
et al., 2010). Cocaine also induces the secretion of TGF-β, which 
has been linked to the observation that cocaine exposure enhances 
replication of the HIV-1 virus in human PBMC (Chao et al., 1991; 
Peterson et al., 1991). A proteomic analysis conducted in human 
DCs treated ex vivo with cocaine for 48 hours demonstrated 
increased expression of NF-κB (Reynolds et al., 2009b), which 
could contribute to increased expression of various cytokines or 
chemokines. Cocaine causes an increased HIV viral burden in a 
human PBL-SCID animal model that is mediated through sigma 
1 (σ1) receptors, which are informally referred to as psychoactive 
drug receptors (Roth et al., 2005). Although the function and role 
of σ1 receptors still remain to be elucidated, additional studies also 
suggest that cocaine effects are mediated through these receptors 
(Maurice and Romieu, 2004; Cabral, 2006). Cocaine, and particu-
larly, crack, has been associated with HIV pathogenesis, which is 
likely due to cocaine/crack-induced suppression of T-cell function 
(Pandhare et al., 2014; Addai et al., 2015).
Methamphetamine  Methamphetamine is a stimulant that is simi-
lar to amphetamine, although highly addictive and its use has been 
growing over the past several years. An investigation into the immu-
notoxicity of methamphetamine showed that methamphetamine sup-
presses the anti-sRBC AFC response, IgG production, and mitogenic 
stimulation of T-cell proliferation following oral administration in 
mice. Even more striking is the suppression of GM-CSF-stimulated 
bone marrow colony growth by methamphetamine (In et al., 2005). 
These results indicate suppression of both CMI and humoral immu-
nity in vivo following methamphetamine administration.
In human T cells, methamphetamine treatment ex vivo induces 
ROS and increases intracellular calcium (Potula et al., 2010). 
Consistent with this, ex vivo treatment of human DCs with meth-
amphetamine induces MEK, which activates MAPKs (Reynolds 
et al., 2009a). Both of these studies demonstrate that in the absence 
of immune stimulation, acute methamphetamine treatment causes 
biochemical changes consistent with cellular activation. A very 
detailed analysis of T-cell subsets altered by methamphetamine in 
LCMV-treated mice revealed that methamphetamine alters the co-
expression of IFN-γ, TNF-α, and TGF-β, especially in CD4+ cells 
(Sriram et al., 2016). In mice treated in vivo with methamphetamine 
or in methamphetamine-using patients, plasma IL-6 is suppressed 
and IL-10 is increased, suggesting immune suppression following 
chronic methamphetamine use (Loftis et al., 2011).
Ethanol  Ethanol exposure has been studied both in alcoholic 
patients and in animal models of binge drinking. In humans, alco-
holism is associated with an increased incidence of, and mortality 
from, pulmonary infection (reviewed by Simet and Sisson [2015]). 
There is also an increased incidence of bacterial infection and 
spontaneous bacteremia in alcoholics with cirrhosis of the liver

Toxic Responses of the Immune System
Chapter 12
687
(reviewed by Szabo and Saha [2015]). A consistent finding in abus-
ers of ethanol is the significant change in PBMC. In animal models, 
this is observed as depletion of T and B cells in the spleen and 
the T cells in the thymus, particularly CD4+ CD8+ cells. The latter 
effect may be related in part to increased levels of corticosteroids, 
particularly in females (Han et al., 1993; Glover et al., 2011). T-cell 
activation, as assessed by CD69 expression, was suppressed by 
alcohol treatment in a sepsis model (Margoles et al., 2016). In a 
binge-drinking animal model, ethanol suppresses innate immunity 
through impairment of TLR3 signaling in peritoneal macrophages, 
correlating with suppression of pro-inflammatory cytokines (Pruett 
et al., 2004b). In addition to suppression of TLR3, ethanol sup-
presses signaling through other TLRs, contributing to pleiotro-
pic effects of ethanol on innate immunity (Pruett et al., 2004a). 
Moreover, signaling alterations via TLRs depends on whether the 
alcohol exposure is acute or chronic (Dai and Pruett, 2006). The lat-
ter might account for the paradoxical observation that alcohol pro-
duces alcoholic liver disease characterized by inflammation leading 
to steatosis, fibrosis, and cirrhosis (Neuman et al., 2015).
Ultraviolet Radiation
Ultraviolet radiation (UVR), especially mid-range UVB (290 to 340 
nm), is an important environmental factor affecting human health 
with both beneficial effects including vitamin D production, tan-
ning, and adaptation to UV, and adverse effects including sunburn, 
skin cancer, and ocular damage. Most, if not all, of the chemicals, 
drugs, and other materials discussed in this chapter were selected 
because they have been associated with immunotoxic effects, and 
because there is the chance for human exposure. Indeed, UVR has 
also been demonstrated to modulate immune responses in ani-
mals and humans (Garssen and van Loveren, 2001; Murphy, 2009; 
Ullrich and Byrne, 2012; Damiani and Ullrich, 2016; Gonzalez 
Maglio et al., 2016; Prasad and Katiyar, 2017). While UV-induced 
immunomodulation has been shown to have some beneficial effects 
on some skin diseases, such as psoriasis, and has been demonstrated 
to impair some allergic and autoimmune diseases in both animals 
and humans, UV-induced immunomodulation can also lead to sev-
eral adverse health consequences, including a pivotal role during 
the process of skin carcinogenesis. Kripke (1981) provided the 
first evidence that UVR is immunosuppressive. UV-induced skin 
tumors were removed and transplanted into normal age- and sex-
matched syngeneic recipient mice. Interestingly, the tumors did 
not grow in normal mice, and progressive tumor growth was only 
seen when the tumors were transplanted into recipient mice that 
were immunosuppressed. These results were explained by pro-
posing that exposure to UVR had two effects: induction of skin 
tumors and induction of immune suppression. Studies have shown 
that these two events are related based on a series of observations 
(Ullrich, 2007b; Damiani and Ullrich, 2016; Prasad and Katiyar, 
2017). First, UVR-induced keratinocyte-derived platelet-activating 
factor plays a role in the induction of immunosuppression. Second, 
cis-urocanic acid, a skin-derived immunosuppressive compound, 
mediates immune suppression by binding to serotonin receptors 
on target cells. Finally, studies showed that blocking the binding 
of these compounds to their receptors not only suppresses UVR-
induced immune suppression, but also interferes with skin cancer 
induction (Sreevidya et al., 2008; Zhang et al., 2008).
There have been a number of studies to further characterize 
the mechanism of action for UV-induced immunomodulation. The 
first step is the absorption of UV photons by chromophores, so-
called “photoreceptors,” such as DNA and urocanic acid (Garssen 
et al., 1997). As a consequence of UV absorption by chromophores, 
epidermal and dermal cells, including keratinocytes, melanocytes, 
Langerhans cells, mast cells, dermal fibroblasts, endothelial cells, as 
well as skin-infiltrating cells (i.e., granulocytes and macrophages), 
produce and/or release many immunoregulatory mediators, includ-
ing cytokines, chemokines, and neurohormones (Sleijffers et al., 
2004). The mediators include both pro- and anti-inflammatory cyto-
kines, such as TNF-α, IL-1, IL-6, IL-10, IL-12, and IL-17, which 
can modify directly or indirectly the function of APCs (Achachi et 
al., 2015; Li et al., 2015b; Prasad and Katiyar, 2017). Langerhans 
cells, the major APC in the skin, change phenotypically and func-
tionally, which ultimately impacts the activity of T cells at the 
time of antigen presentation, both locally and systemically. One 
early explanation for UV-induced immunomodulation is that UVR 
induced a switch from a predominantly Th1 response (favoring 
DTH responses) to a Th2 response (favoring antibody responses). 
This hypothesis was supported by initial findings of altered cyto-
kine secretion patterns indicative of a Th1 to Th2 switch (Araneo 
et al., 1989; Simon et al., 1990). Indeed, the majority of studies 
dealing with the effects of UVR indicate that Th1-mediated immune 
responses are especially sensitive to UV exposure. However, as 
noted above, UVR is associated with suppression of certain allergic 
and autoimmune reactions. Indeed, more recent studies demonstrate 
that Ig isotypes that are linked to either Th1 or Th2 cells can be 
suppressed by UVR and that UV exposure not only impairs Th1 
responses, but also impairs humoral immunity by inhibiting TFH and 
inducing Bregs that secrete IL-10 (Sleijffers et al., 2004; Byrne and 
Halliday, 2005; Gonzalez Maglio et al., 2016).
Schwartz (2008) summarized 25 years of studies by noting 
that in contrast to the general immunosuppression associated with 
the use of conventional immunosuppressive drugs, UVR suppresses 
the immune system in an antigen-specific fashion via the induc-
tion of tolerance. Several investigators have noted that the immu-
notolerance is mostly mediated via Tregs induced by UVR, and that 
induction of Tregs, expressing CD4 and CD25, is an active process, 
which requires antigen presentation by UV damage. Once activated 
in an antigen-specific manner, these Tregs can suppress immune 
responses in an IL-10-dependent manner. Ullrich (2007a) noted that 
this model is consistent with the fact that UVR is absorbed in the 
upper layers of the skin, does not penetrate into the underlying tis-
sues and internal organs, and that T cells are not directly targeted by 
UVR in vivo because few T cells are found in normal skin (Ullrich, 
2007a). Mast cells also appear to be key regulators of the systemic 
immunosuppression induced by UVR in that mast cells regulate 
both innate and adaptive immune responses through the cytokines 
they secrete and the generation of immunosuppressive cells (e.g., 
Treg and Breg) (Byrne et al., 2008; Chacon-Salinas et al., 2011; 
Ullrich and Byrne, 2012).
Therapeutic Drugs
The recent explosion of knowledge regarding the function and 
regulation of the immune system (at the cellular, biochemical, and 
molecular levels) has provided investigators with a relatively new 
avenue for specific drug development. The following discussion 
focuses on those small molecule drugs used primarily for modu-
lating the immune system: anti-inflammatory drugs, immunosup-
pressants (corticosteroids are described in the section “Natural and 
Synthetic Hormones”), and AIDS therapeutics.
Anti-inflammatory Drugs  Anti-inflammatory drugs include 
nonselective and selective nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs 
(NSAIDs), which suppress the production of pro-inflamma-
tory soluble factors, such as prostaglandins and thromboxanes. 
Nonselective NSAIDs are a large class of drugs that reversibly

688
Target Organ Toxicity
Unit IV
inhibit both isoforms of cyclooxygenase (COX-1 and COX-2). The 
COX-2 enzyme, in particular, is induced in response to inflamma-
tory cytokines and mediators and therefore represents an attrac-
tive target to combat inflammatory diseases. However, due to an 
increased risk of cardiovascular effects in some patients (reviewed 
by Grosser et al. [2006]), which is a side effect to varying degrees 
for all NSAIDs, the only COX-2 selective inhibitor currently 
approved for clinical use is celecoxib (celebrex). Aspirin, like non-
selective NSAIDs, inhibits COX-1 and COX-2 enzymes but inhibi-
tion is irreversible due to covalent binding of aspirin by acetylation 
to a serine residue in the COX enzyme. Aspirin is especially effec-
tive as an antiplatelet because platelets possess little biosynthesiz-
ing capacity and therefore aspirin will inhibit COX for the life of 
the platelet (8 to 11 days).
Immunosuppressive 
Agents  Several 
immunosuppressive 
drugs are efficacious simply due to their ability to impair cellu-
lar proliferation, because proliferation is required for lymphocyte 
clonal expansion and, subsequently, differentiation. More recently, 
drugs that inhibit specific intracellular proteins critical in the acti-
vation of the immune response have been approved.
Cyclophosphamide (Cytoxan, CTX)  CTX, which was originally 
developed as an antineoplastic drug, is the prototypical member 
of a class of drugs known as alkylating agents. Upon entering the 
cell, the inactive drug is metabolically cleaved into phosphoramide 
mustard, a powerful DNA alkylating agent that leads to blockade 
of cell replication, and acrolein, a compound known to primarily
